### [305] [The Verification Tax: Fundamental Limits of AI Auditing in the Rare-Error Regime](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12951)
*Jason Z Wang*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Calibration verification for modern LMs is fundamentally limited by a minimax-rate noise floor; self-evaluation is uninformative, and verification cost grows with model and pipeline depth, necessitating reporting verification floors and active querying.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Challenge the prevailing belief that post-temperature-scaling ECE improvements imply true calibration; provide a theory-backed limit on calibration-error estimation under model error and empirical validation across multiple benchmarks to inform evaluation practice.

Method: Theoretical minimax analysis of calibration-error estimation with model error epsilon, deriving the rate Theta((L epsilon/m)^{1/3}); establish a sharp phase transition near mepsilon ~ 1; analyze the impact of active querying on the Lipschitz constant and estimation vs detection; derive exponential verification cost with pipeline depth L^K; validate across five benchmarks (MMLU, TruthfulQA, ARC-Challenge, HellaSwag, WinoGrande; ~27k items) using 6 LLMs from 5 families (8B-405B), 27 benchmark-model pairs with logprob-based confidence, 95% bootstrap CIs, and permutation tests.

Result: Minimax rate for calibration-error estimation is Theta((L epsilon/m)^{1/3}); self-evaluation without labels yields zero information about calibration; phase transition at mepsilon ≈ 1 below which miscalibration is undetectable; active querying removes the Lipschitz constraint, reducing estimation to detection; verification cost grows as L^K with pipeline depth; across frontier models, 23% of pairwise comparisons are indistinguishable from noise, and 80% of self-evaluation pairs are non-significant.

Conclusion: Calibration verification must report verification floors; prioritize active querying as gains approach benchmark resolution; the results reveal fundamental limits to precise calibration verification as models scale, calling for revised evaluation protocols and reporting standards.

Abstract: The most cited calibration result in deep learning -- post-temperature-scaling ECE of 0.012 on CIFAR-100 (Guo et al., 2017) -- is below the statistical noise floor. We prove this is not a failure of the experiment but a law: the minimax rate for estimating calibration error with model error rate epsilon is Theta((Lepsilon/m)^{1/3}), and no estimator can beat it. This "verification tax" implies that as AI models improve, verifying their calibration becomes fundamentally harder -- with the same exponent in opposite directions. We establish four results that contradict standard evaluation practice: (1) self-evaluation without labels provides exactly zero information about calibration, bounded by a constant independent of compute; (2) a sharp phase transition at mepsilon approx 1 below which miscalibration is undetectable; (3) active querying eliminates the Lipschitz constant, collapsing estimation to detection; (4) verification cost grows exponentially with pipeline depth at rate L^K. We validate across five benchmarks (MMLU, TruthfulQA, ARC-Challenge, HellaSwag, WinoGrande; ~27,000 items) with 6 LLMs from 5 families (8B-405B parameters, 27 benchmark-model pairs with logprob-based confidence), 95% bootstrap CIs, and permutation tests. Self-evaluation non-significance holds in 80% of pairs. Across frontier models, 23% of pairwise comparisons are indistinguishable from noise, implying that credible calibration claims must report verification floors and prioritize active querying once gains approach benchmark resolution.

Abstract: The Sauer-Shelah-Perles Lemma is a cornerstone of combinatorics and learning theory, bounding the size of a binary hypothesis class in terms of its Vapnik-Chervonenkis (VC) dimension. For classes of functions over a $k$-ary alphabet, namely the multiclass setting, the Natarajan dimension has long served as an analogue of VC dimension, yet the corresponding Sauer-type bounds are suboptimal for alphabet sizes $k>2$.
  In this work, we establish a sharp Sauer inequality for multiclass and list prediction. Our bound is expressed in terms of the Daniely--Shalev-Shwartz (DS) dimension, and more generally with its extension, the list-DS dimension -- the combinatorial parameters that characterize multiclass and list PAC learnability. Our bound is tight for every alphabet size $k$, list size $\ell$, and dimension value, replacing the exponential dependence on $\ell$ in the Natarajan-based bound by the optimal polynomial dependence, and improving the dependence on $k$ as well. Our proof uses the polynomial method. In contrast to the classical VC case, where several direct combinatorial proofs are known, we are not aware of any purely combinatorial proof in the DS setting. This motivates several directions for future research, which are discussed in the paper.
  As consequences, we obtain improved sample complexity upper bounds for list PAC learning and for uniform convergence of list predictors, sharpening the recent results of Charikar et al.~(STOC~2023), Hanneke et al.~(COLT~2024), and Brukhim et al.~(NeurIPS~2024).

### [308] [Lightning OPD: Efficient Post-Training for Large Reasoning Models with Offline On-Policy Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.13010)

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Lightning OPD enables offline on-policy distillation by enforcing teacher consistency, achieving the same optimum as standard OPD with gradient bias mitigation and a 4x speedup; shows state-of-the-art results on math reasoning and code generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: OPD requires a live teacher inference server, causing substantial infrastructure overhead. Offline variants often fail due to a previously overlooked issue—teacher consistency—between the supervisor model used for fine-tuning and the OPD teacher.

Method: Precompute teacher log-probabilities over SFT rollouts and enforce the same teacher model for both supervised fine-tuning and OPD (teacher consistency). Analyze irreducible gradient bias when inconsistency exists, propose Lightning OPD to eliminate live teacher servers, and prove equivalence of optima under consistency with bounded gradient discrepancy and implicit regularization.

Result: Under teacher consistency, Lightning OPD shares the same optimum as standard OPD, with bounded gradient discrepancy and an implicit regularization that curbs policy drift. Empirically, it achieves state-of-the-art performance on math reasoning and code generation, e.g., 69.9% on AIME 2024 starting from a SFT-initialized Qwen3-8B-Base in 30 GPU hours, about a 4x speedup over standard OPD.

Abstract: On-policy distillation (OPD) has emerged as an efficient post-training paradigm for large language models. However, standard OPD requires a live teacher inference server throughout training, resulting in substantial infrastructure overhead. In this work, we investigate whether on-policy distillation can be performed offline. A natural approach is to precompute teacher log-probabilities once over SFT rollouts and reuse them during training. In practice, however, this offline variant fails to reliably match the performance of standard OPD. To understand this discrepancy, we identify a previously overlooked condition that is critical for any OPD pipeline, which we term teacher consistency. This condition requires that the same teacher model be used for both supervised fine-tuning and OPD. We show that violating teacher consistency introduces an irreducible gradient bias, causing both offline and online OPD to converge to a suboptimal fixed point regardless of training duration. Building on this insight, we propose Lightning OPD, an offline on-policy distillation framework that enforces teacher consistency by precomputing teacher log-probabilities over SFT rollouts. This design eliminates the need for a live teacher server entirely. We further show that, under teacher consistency, Lightning OPD shares the same optimum as standard OPD, with bounded gradient discrepancy and an implicit regularization effect that helps prevent policy drift. Extensive experiments on mathematical reasoning and code generation demonstrate that Lightning OPD achieves state-of-the-art performance with significantly improved efficiency. Starting from an SFT-initialized Qwen3-8B-Base model, Lightning OPD reaches 69.9% on AIME 2024 in just 30 GPU hours, achieving a 4.0x speedup over standard OPD and substantially lowering the barrier to entry for academic research on LLM post-training.

</details>


### [309] [Rethinking On-Policy Distillation of Large Language Models: Phenomenology, Mechanism, and Recipe](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.13016)
*Yaxuan Li,Yuxin Zuo,Bingxiang He,Jinqian Zhang,Chaojun Xiao,Cheng Qian,Tianyu Yu,Huan-ang Gao,Wenkai Yang,Zhiyuan Liu,Ning Ding*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: OPD succeeds when student and teacher share compatible thinking patterns and the teacher offers genuinely new capabilities; even high-scoring OPD can fail if these conditions aren’t met. Same-family 1.5B/7B teachers look distributionally identical to the student, while OPD relies on progressive alignment to a small set of high-probability tokens. The paper proposes off-policy cold start and teacher-aligned prompts to recover failing OPD, and cautions that dense token-level rewards may hinder long-horizon distillation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand the training dynamics and failure modes of on-policy distillation (OPD) for large language models, identify the conditions for success, and propose practical remedies and limitations.

Method: Systematic investigation combining weak-to-strong reverse distillation experiments across same-family teacher sizes (e.g., 1.5B and 7B), token-level probing of alignment, and evaluation of practical strategies (off-policy cold start, teacher-aligned prompts).

Result: OPD succeeds when thinking patterns align and the teacher offers new capabilities; success is marked by progressive alignment on high-probability tokens at student-visited states and a small shared token set that carries most probability mass (97–99%). Same-family teachers are distributionally indistinguishable from the student from the student’s perspective. Two remediation strategies are proposed: off-policy cold start and teacher-aligned prompt selection. OPD’s apparent dense token-level reward incurs costs, raising scalability concerns for long-horizon distillation.

Conclusion: OPD has identifiable success criteria and practical remedies, but it may not scale to long horizons due to the cost of dense token-level rewards and potential lack of genuinely new capabilities from similarly distributed teachers; future work should address scalable long-horizon distillation and deeper analysis of thinking-pattern compatibility.

Abstract: On-policy distillation (OPD) has become a core technique in the post-training of large language models, yet its training dynamics remain poorly understood. This paper provides a systematic investigation of OPD dynamics and mechanisms. We first identify that two conditions govern whether OPD succeeds or fails: (i) the student and teacher should share compatible thinking patterns; and (ii) even with consistent thinking patterns and higher scores, the teacher must offer genuinely new capabilities beyond what the student has seen during training. We validate these findings through weak-to-strong reverse distillation, showing that same-family 1.5B and 7B teachers are distributionally indistinguishable from the student's perspective. Probing into the token-level mechanism, we show that successful OPD is characterized by progressive alignment on high-probability tokens at student-visited states, a small shared token set that concentrates most of the probability mass (97%-99%). We further propose two practical strategies to recover failing OPD: off-policy cold start and teacher-aligned prompt selection. Finally, we show that OPD's apparent free lunch of dense token-level reward comes at a cost, raising the question of whether OPD can scale to long-horizon distillation.

</details>


### [310] [CLAD: Efficient Log Anomaly Detection Directly on Compressed Representations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.13024)
*Benzhao Tang,Shiyu Yang*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A deep learning framework CLAD performs log anomaly detection directly on compressed byte streams, achieving state-of-the-art F1 while removing decompression/parsing overhead.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Explosive growth of system logs and the overhead of decompressing/parsing before LAD; need streaming, efficient anomaly detection that works on compressed data.

Method: A dedicated architecture: dilated convolutional byte encoder to extract multi-scale byte patterns; hybrid Transformer–mLSTM for sequence modeling; four-way aggregation pooling; two-stage training with masked pre-training and focal-contrastive fine-tuning to handle class imbalance.

Result: Evaluated on five datasets with average F1 0.9909, beating best baseline by 2.72 percentage points; reduces overhead by avoiding decompression; generalizes to structured streaming compressors.

Conclusion: Demonstrates that LAD can be effectively performed on compressed streams with high accuracy and low overhead; CLAD provides a robust, generalizable framework for streaming log anomaly detection.

Abstract: The explosive growth of system logs makes streaming compression essential, yet existing log anomaly detection (LAD) methods incur severe pre-processing overhead by requiring full decompression and parsing. We introduce CLAD, the first deep learning framework to perform LAD directly on compressed byte streams. CLAD bypasses these bottlenecks by exploiting a key insight: normal logs compress into regular byte patterns, while anomalies systematically disrupt them. To extract these multi-scale deviations from opaque bytes, we propose a purpose-built architecture integrating a dilated convolutional byte encoder, a hybrid Transformer--mLSTM, and four-way aggregation pooling. This is coupled with a two-stage training strategy of masked pre-training and focal-contrastive fine-tuning to effectively handle severe class imbalance. Evaluated across five datasets, CLAD achieves a state-of-the-art average F1-score of 0.9909 and outperforms the best baseline by 2.72 percentage points. It delivers superior accuracy while completely eliminating decompression and parsing overheads, offering a robust solution that generalizes to structured streaming compressors.

</details>

### [311] [Topology Understanding of B-Spline Surface/Surface Intersection with Mapper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.12631)
*Chenming Gao,Hongwei Lin,Gengchen Li*

Main category: cs.CG

TL;DR: Mapper-based topology extraction for B-spline surface intersections improves robustness and correctness in subdivision-based pipelines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Subdivision-based intersection of B-spline surfaces is robust but recovering the correct intersection topology is challenging; errors in topology can yield incorrect intersections. A reliable topology determination is essential for correctness and downstream CAD operations.

Method: Apply Mapper, a topological data analysis tool, to the set of subdivision-derived intersection candidates. Build a cover of the intersection domain, map to a lower-dimensional representation, and construct a nerve/graph that encodes connectivity to reveal components, branches, and singular configurations. Integrate this topology with the B-spline parameter space and handle singular/degenerate cases efficiently to produce a consistent intersection topology.

Result: Experimental evaluation on diverse B-spline surface pairs demonstrates robustness to complex topologies and accuracy in topology reconstruction, with favorable validation against ground-truth topologies and improved reliability over purely subdivision-based approaches.

Conclusion: Mapper-based topology determination effectively supports robust and correct topology recovery for B-spline surface intersections within subdivision-based pipelines and is scalable to complex configurations, with potential applicability to other surface-intersection problems.

Abstract: In the realm of computer-aided design (CAD) software, the intersection of B-spline surfaces stands as a fundamental operation. Despite the extensive history of surface intersection algorithms, the challenge of handling complex intersection topologies persists. While subdivision algorithms have demonstrated strong robustness in computing surface/surface intersection and are capable of addressing singular cases, determining the topology of the intersection obtained through these methods is a key factor for calculating correct intersection, and remains a difficult issue. To address this challenge, we propose a Mapper-based method for determining the topology of the intersection between two B-spline surfaces. Our algorithm is designed to efficiently handle various common and complex intersection topologies. Experimental results verify the robustness and topological correctness of this method.

</details>
